    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.6sym92tms9tb"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Academic papers and
            presentations</span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Recovering meaningful artifacts from the registry during digital forensics is also a
            problem known in academia. To find relevant works, I often begin by typing the titles of a few known papers
            in </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://scholar.google.com/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260944494&amp;usg=AOvVaw0YtTbdmRySGLWusI2xMggq">Google
                Scholar</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, and then delve into a breadth-first search
            of their bibliographies. Here&#39;s what I managed to find pertaining to the registry:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_k83m7zl0bnts-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1742287608000297&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260945439&amp;usg=AOvVaw2WSEbMsB3gaE4Wr_ah3RJg">Forensic
                    analysis of the Windows registry in memory</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2008), Brendan Dolan-Gavitt &ndash; a paper detailing
                techniques for extracting and analyzing Windows registry data from physical memory dumps.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=http://sentinelchicken.com/research/registry_recovery/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260946172&amp;usg=AOvVaw2vb0OH8A0LAHei4k0JKzlw">Recovering
                    deleted data from the Windows registry</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2008), Timothy D. Morgan &ndash; a paper and accompanying
                slide deck that examine deleted registry data structures in NT-based Windows systems, propose an
                algorithm for their recovery, and introduce the RegLookup tool to implement this recovery
                process.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=http://sentinelchicken.com/data/JolantaThomassenDISSERTATION.pdf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260946926&amp;usg=AOvVaw2dMGxYEU66bxVK7qe0U0aJ">Forensic
                    analysis of unallocated space in Windows registry hive files</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2008), Jolanta Thomassen &ndash; a 63-page MSc dissertation
                demonstrating the feasibility of recovering deleted or updated Windows registry keys from unallocated
                space within hive files.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://web.archive.org/web/20090529202753/http://amnesia.gtisc.gatech.edu/~moyix/suzibandit.ltd.uk/MSc/Registry%2520Structure%2520-%2520Main%2520V4.pdf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260947881&amp;usg=AOvVaw0Us3lO7Q4JHc_08-oFGvdW">The
                    internal structure of the Windows Registry</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2009), Peter Norris &ndash; a 144-page MSc thesis focusing on
                the reconstruction of damaged registry files, analysis of historical states, and the extraction of
                standalone forensic evidence from dispersed fragments.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=http://www.sentinelchicken.com/data/TheWindowsNTRegistryFileFormat.pdf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260948654&amp;usg=AOvVaw2r5dgqdcU9xRI8HULMDHit">The
                    Windows NT Registry File Format</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2009),
                Timothy D. Morgan &ndash; a concise paper providing a comprehensive description of the regf format data
                structures.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.i.u-tokyo.ac.jp/edu/training/ss/lecture/new-documents/Lectures/09-Registry/Registry.pdf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260949481&amp;usg=AOvVaw2rR2BkNKB0fcaoXBqAsH4L">Windows
                    Kernel Internals: NT Registry Implementation</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2009), David B. Probert &ndash; a slide deck discussing the
                registry internals through the lens of the Windows kernel, offering a unique perspective of a Windows
                kernel developer.</span></li>
    </ul>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.a1wpxmdreh9q"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Open-source
            software</span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>To paraphrase a famous saying, </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">source code is
            worth a thousand words</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">. Sometimes it is far easier to grasp
            a concept or design by looking straight at code instead of reading an English explanation. And while the
            canonical implementation of the registry is the Windows kernel, a number of open-source projects have been
            developed over the years to operate on registry hives. They are typically either based on regf format
            analysis performed by the developer itself, or on existing documentation and other open-source tools. The
            three main reasons for their existence are a) computer forensics, b) emulating Windows behavior on other
            host platforms, c) directly accessing the SAM hive to change/reset local user credentials. Whatever the
            reason, such projects may prove useful in better understanding the internal hive format, and help in
            building proof-of-concept hives if necessary. A list of all the relevant open-source libraries and utilities
            I have found is shown below:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_oiksgmwcrt8b-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/libyal/libregf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260951444&amp;usg=AOvVaw1wbD9_bHavG5GJmZhtapst">libregf</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; a library written in C with Python bindings,</span>
        </li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/libguestfs/hivex&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260952045&amp;usg=AOvVaw0hmo--_R1EL0lFfFcu0zrq">hivex</a></span><span>&nbsp;&ndash;
                a library written in C as part of the </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://libguestfs.org/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260952382&amp;usg=AOvVaw3crw_JVJLgs5MYNBi7s7MI">libguestfs</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;project, with bindings for OCaml, Perl, Python and
                Ruby,</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/reactos/reactos/tree/master/sdk/lib/cmlib&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260952995&amp;usg=AOvVaw2sqQNPUUMV3NWrRZ--xApY">cmlib</a></span><span>&nbsp;&ndash;
                a module implemented in C as part of </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://reactos.org/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260953315&amp;usg=AOvVaw1KlgNHuZha4B6baXWahSIk">ReactOS</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, which closely resembles the Windows implementation,</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://pogostick.net/~pnh/ntpasswd/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260953855&amp;usg=AOvVaw0JHZPpnmH0ZwyCk8v4aaCG">chntpw</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(The Offline Windows Password Editor) &ndash; a tool developed
                in C between 1997-2014 to manage Windows user passwords offline directly in the SAM hive. The
                registry-related code is located in ntreg.c (regf parser) and reged.c (a basic registry editor),</span>
        </li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.samba.org/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260954449&amp;usg=AOvVaw0M9HM1tM-l50d645uRf2c2">Samba</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; the Samba project includes yet another implementation
                of the Windows registry (under source3/registry and source4/lib/registry),</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/mkorman90/regipy&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260954958&amp;usg=AOvVaw0RhCPn3TAD4ARIeo1doAD5">regipy</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; a Python registry hive parsing library and
                accompanying tools,</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/msuhanov/yarp/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260955438&amp;usg=AOvVaw12DN7_ee88FD4Xyuimn07r">yarp</a></span><span>&nbsp;&ndash;
                literally </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">yet another registry parser</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(in Python),</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/EricZimmerman/Registry&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260956158&amp;usg=AOvVaw2ZaptRk23JnoVLBVoWsTHc">Registry</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; a hive parser written in C#,</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/ColinFinck/nt-hive&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260956686&amp;usg=AOvVaw0y1NmJXYcAXA4duUe3gaja">nt-hive</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; a hive parser written in Rust (with read-only
                capabilities),</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/strozfriedberg/notatin&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260957252&amp;usg=AOvVaw1tg1CYodxFTex-VHDv-AfP">Notatin</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;&ndash; another hive parser written in Rust, including Python
                bindings and helper binaries.</span></li>
    </ul>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Lastly, at the time of this writing, simply searching for some internal kernel
            function names on </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260957999&amp;usg=AOvVaw1GBW5kCguqYC_YKYM2U5jh">GitHub</a></span><span>&nbsp;might
            reveal how certain functionality was implemented in Windows itself 20+ years ago.</span></p>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.1mkmgps19tb5"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">SDK Headers</span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Header files distributed with </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Software Development Kits are an interesting case, because on one hand
            they are an official resource with information that Microsoft intends the developers to use, but on the
            other &ndash; they are a bit more concealed, as online documentation isn&#39;t always kept up to date with
            regards to their contents. We can thus explore their local copies on disk and sometimes find artifacts
            (function declarations, structure definitions, comments) that are not publicly documented online. Some of
            the headers most relevant to the registry are:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_pv42b0usiw40-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/winreg/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260959782&amp;usg=AOvVaw0ZBrSIqPhtAe3inJ529PzZ">winreg.h</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(user-mode) &ndash; the primary registry header on the list,
                containing the prototypes of functions and structures from the official Registry API.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/ddi/wdm/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260960477&amp;usg=AOvVaw3cDD1enjMeMLlzYsmAf2aJ">wdm.h</a></span><span>&nbsp;(kernel-mode)
                &ndash; specifies a number of interesting constants/flags and types used by the system call interface of
                the registry, for example hive load flags (third argument of </span><span>NtLoadKey2</span><span>, such
                as </span><span>REG_LOAD_HIVE_OPEN_HANDLE</span><span>&nbsp;etc.) or key/value query structures
                (</span><span>KEY_TRUST_INFORMATION</span><span>, </span><span>KEY_VALUE_LAYER_INFORMATION</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, etc.).</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/ddi/ntddk/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260960889&amp;usg=AOvVaw0niS_TzAw-hM92Z2KvXHc0">ntddk.h</a></span><span>&nbsp;(kernel-mode)
                &ndash; contains some types not found elsewhere, e.g. </span><span>KEY_LAYER_INFORMATION</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/winnt/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260961095&amp;usg=AOvVaw1E28iy16B4PgEF8POYpJOx">winnt.h</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(user-mode) &ndash; mostly equivalent to wdm.h.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/win32/api/winternl/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260961259&amp;usg=AOvVaw1d0osga5nxpsMkcR3PCahf">winternl.h</a></span><span>&nbsp;(user-mode)
                &ndash; contains the declarations of some registry-related system calls
                (</span><span>NtRenameKey</span><span>, </span><span>NtSetInformationKey</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">).</span></li>
    </ul>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.3cvqpubk4o52"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Security research</span>
    </h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Learning about prior security research can be
            especially useful when starting a new project yourself. Not only does it often reveal deep technical details
            about the target, but it also comes from like-minded professionals who look at the code through a security
            lens, and may inspire ideas of further weaknesses or areas that require more attention. When it comes to the
            registry, I think that relatively little work has been done in the public space compared to its high
            complexity and the pivotal role it plays in the Windows operating system. Nevertheless, there were some
            materials that I found extremely insightful, especially those by my colleague James Forshaw from Project
            Zero. The full list of security-relevant resources I have managed to gather on this topic is shown below
            (including some references to my own publications from the past):</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_l4cahwt7oweg-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://j00ru.vexillium.org/slides/2010/confidence.pdf&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260961763&amp;usg=AOvVaw0_-hjsGRJPbe3zOg5sMppI">Case
                    study of recent Windows vulnerabilities</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2010), Gynvael Coldwind, Mateusz Jurczyk &ndash; a
                presentation on several security bugs Gynvael and I found during our brief registry research in
                2009/2010.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.fortinet.com/blog/threat-research/microsoft-kernel-integer-overflow-vulnerability&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260961959&amp;usg=AOvVaw3G2wknr8OBH7aIgwdYiQF-">Microsoft
                    Kernel Integer Overflow Vulnerability</a></span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2016), Honggang Ren &ndash; a write-up on CVE-2016-0070, a
                Windows kernel vulnerability in the loading of malformed hive files.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://bugs.chromium.org/p/project-zero/issues/list?q%3Did%253A873%252C874%252C876%252C993%26can%3D1&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260962143&amp;usg=AOvVaw3YT7h6ZbgxaMmg-35a2MA8">Project
                    Zero bug tracker</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2016), James Forshaw,
                Mateusz Jurczyk &ndash; four bug reports submitted to Microsoft as a result of naive registry hive
                fuzzing.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://bugs.chromium.org/p/project-zero/issues/list?q%3Did%253A123%252C215%252C865%252C870%252C871%252C872%252C875%252C1492%252C1494%252C1554%252C1599%252C1786%252C1795%252C2047%252C2048%252C2120%252C2129%26can%3D1&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260962359&amp;usg=AOvVaw3wqrPPu_BmLT9VwglTof1R">Project
                    Zero bug tracker</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;(2014-2020), James
                Forshaw &ndash; 17 vulnerabilities related to the registry discovered by James, many of them are logic
                issues at the intersection of registry and other system mechanisms (security impersonation, file
                system).</span></li>
    </ul>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.9h6akcjvwdqx"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Books</span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>For a 20+ year old codebase such as the registry, it is expected that some resources
            covering it in the early days were published on paper rather than on the Internet. For this reason, part of
            my standard routine is to search </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://books.google.com/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260962643&amp;usg=AOvVaw0-UAZXVeUgJjNJEhCqVwuZ">Google
                Books</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;for various technical terms and keywords
            related to the specific technology and see what pop ups. For the registry, these could be e.g.
            &quot;regedit&quot;, &quot;regf&quot;, &quot;hbin&quot;, &quot;LOG1&quot;, &quot;RegCreateKey&quot;,
            &quot;NtCreateKey&quot;, &quot;HvAllocateCell&quot;, &quot;\Registry\Machine&quot;, &quot;key control
            block&quot; and so on. In some cases this yields books with unique, strictly technical information, while in
            others the most insightful part is the historical perspective and being able to see how the given technology
            was perceived soon after it first came out. And sometimes the value of the book is a complete surprise until
            it arrives in the mailbox, as it is neither offered for sale as an ebook nor has preview available in Google
            Books, and so a hard copy is required.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">The books that I found which are either fully or
            partially dedicated to the Windows registry are (latest to oldest):</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_v97v167fex95-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>&#128273; </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Windows_Internals_Part_2/L8UIvAEACAAJ?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260963035&amp;usg=AOvVaw3nOf2wp7mYzIK_V4ps1w69">Windows
                    Internals (Part 2, 7th Edition)</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by Andrea
                Allievi, Alex Ionescu, Mark E. Russinovich, David A. Solomon &ndash; the Windows Internals series is an
                in-depth technical guide that delves into the architecture, components, and underlying mechanisms of the
                operating system. The latest edition, covering Windows 10, features a dedicated 35-page chapter on the
                registry, and explores many technical details that are difficult to find elsewhere. Notably, the
                registry has been covered in the book since Windows Internals 4 (corresponding to Windows XP/Server
                2003), with explanations progressively expanding in subsequent editions. Comparing these chapters could
                be an interesting exercise to observe how the registry has evolved throughout the years.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Windows_Registry_Forensics/BtVtBgAAQBAJ?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260963350&amp;usg=AOvVaw0vMnD5Xl-eQljUT5lPZtTW">Windows
                    Registry Forensics</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by Harlan Carvey</span>
        </li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Microsoft_Windows_Registry_Guide_2nd_Edi/rayHzwEACAAJ?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260963534&amp;usg=AOvVaw3FJBsGCrSlM7MgMMITi5FS">Microsoft
                    Windows Registry Guide</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by Jerry
                Honeycutt</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Managing_the_Windows_NT_Registry/rYIhAQAAIAAJ?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260963707&amp;usg=AOvVaw3QUPD9LwuKFhT4EHvK6qct">Managing
                    the Windows NT Registry</a></span><span>&nbsp;and </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Managing_the_Windows_2000_Registry/36lOt-iJ6kEC?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260963831&amp;usg=AOvVaw0NGhMulnvWz9qNm1EyHJM-">Managing
                    the Windows 2000 Registry</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by Paul E.
                Robichaux</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Inside_the_Registry_for_Microsoft_Window/zDr22AwXWqsC?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260964034&amp;usg=AOvVaw3OqukDCCGGF56KUGWYM4o_">Inside
                    the Registry for Microsoft Windows 95</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by
                G&uuml;nter Born</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                    href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://www.google.com/books/edition/Inside_the_Windows_95_Registry/ZLZQAAAAMAAJ?hl%3Den&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260964265&amp;usg=AOvVaw0BQ_2kprRoXuQ5dygXqFYs">Inside
                    the Windows 95 Registry</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;by Ron
                Petrusha</span></li>
    </ul>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c14 WuvYfpVbPj-c15" id="h.us6slc1s6k2f"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Patents </span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Another useful source of information that may be otherwise difficult to find are
            patents, indexed by </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://patents.google.com/&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260964570&amp;usg=AOvVaw1mNgUsigveI52zhI9nWF5u">Google
                Patents</a></span><span>. A particularly valuable result that I found this way is &#128273; </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://patents.google.com/patent/US20170279678A1/en&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260964681&amp;usg=AOvVaw1A0MmARNgoEH6VuVcCMXLo">Containerized Configuration</a></span><span> (US20170279678A1), Microsoft&#39;s patent from 2016 that thoroughly
            explains the core concepts behind differencing hives and layered keys in registry. These mechanisms are part
            of a new feature introduced in Windows 10 Anniversary Update to better support containerization, but any
            official documentation of how it works is nowhere to be found. The patent is thus a great aid in
            understanding the intricate aspects of this new registry functionality, adding the necessary context and
            helping to make sense of otherwise highly cryptic kernel functions like
        </span><span>CmpPrepareDiscardAndReplaceKcbAndUnbackedHigherLayers</span><span>.</span></p>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.z62hcqmshcd1"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Manual analysis</span>
    </h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">So far, all of the resources we&#39;ve discussed
            were accessible through a web browser, a text editor, or in physical form. But there is another type of
            information source that is equally, if not more, important, and that requires more specialized tooling to
            make sense of it. What I mean by that is the knowledge we can extract from the executable images in Windows
            responsible for handling the registry, both in terms of the &quot;standard&quot; reverse-engineering and
            also fully taking advantage of any helpful artifacts in or around them. I&#39;ll write more about the
            hands-on reversing process in upcoming posts, and now we will turn our attention to those artifacts that
            present us with clear-cut information without the need for deduction.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>On a side note, by far the most essential file to be looking at is </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c21">ntoskrnl.exe</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, the core NT kernel
            image. It contains the entirety of the kernel-space registry implementation and is of interest both from the
            security and functional perspective. I have personally spent 99% of my manual analysis time looking at that
            particular binary, but it&#39;s worth noting that there are a few other executables and libraries related to
            the registry as well:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_hwtq27crp0ns-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">winload.exe
                &ndash; the Windows Boot Loader, which executes before the Windows kernel. One of its responsibilities
                is to load the SYSTEM hive into memory and read some configuration from it, so it includes a partial
                copy of the registry code from ntoskrnl.exe.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">offreg.dll
                &ndash; the Offline Registry Library, which also shares some registry code with the kernel (but executes
                in user-mode).</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">kernelbase.dll &ndash; one of the primary WinAPI libraries,
                implementing a majority of the user-space Registry API.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">ntdll.dll
                &ndash; another core user-mode library which provides a bridge between the Registry API and the kernel
                registry implementation.</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">regsvc.dll
                &ndash; a DLL implementing the Remote Registry Service.</span></li>
    </ul>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Let&#39;s investigate what types of information about the
        </span><span>registry</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;are readily available to us by
            running a disassembler/decompiler. I personally use IDA Pro + Hex-Rays and so the examples below are based
            on them.</span></p>
    <h3 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c18 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.tvk3kd7ke6cy"><span>&#128273; </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c23 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Public symbols (PDB)</span></h3>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Microsoft makes public symbols available for a majority of executable images found in
            C:\Windows, for the benefit of developers and security researchers. By &quot;public&quot; symbols I mean PDB
            files that mainly contain the names of functions found in the binaries, which help in symbolizing system
            stack traces during debugging or in the event of a crash. In the past, the symbols used to be bundled with
            the system installation media or on a separate Resource Kit disc, and later they were available for download
            in the form of pre-packaged archives from the Microsoft website. Both of these channels have been
            deprecated, and currently the only supported way to obtain the symbols is on a per-file basis from the
        </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/debugger/microsoft-public-symbols&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260966122&amp;usg=AOvVaw1CUphRjOVmjkz5MtmvwQ27">Microsoft
                Symbol Server</a></span><span>. The PDB files can be downloaded directly with the official </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/debugger/using-symchk&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260966252&amp;usg=AOvVaw1Y4RO-pSVwSCNJ4Ki_2mhy">SymChk</a></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;tool, or indirectly through software that supports the symbol
            server (e.g. IDA Pro, WinDbg).</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>As for ntoskrnl.exe specifically, its accompanying symbols are one of the most
            invaluable sources of information. As mentioned in an earlier post, the Windows kernel follows a consistent
            naming convention, so we can immediately see which internal routines are related to the registry, and where
            the entry points (registry-related system call handlers) that we might start our analysis from are. It shows
            us the extent of the code we are dealing with (1000+ registry functions) and makes it possible to perform
            analysis such as the one shown in </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://googleprojectzero.blogspot.com/2024/04/the-windows-registry-adventure-2.html&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260966525&amp;usg=AOvVaw0vbPW-Xcfl4W8MAY11qjGy">blog
                post #2</a></span><span>&nbsp;(counting lines of code per system version) out-of-the-box, without doing
            any reverse engineering work. And perhaps most importantly, the function names make it substantially easier
            to reason about the code while doing the actual reversing, especially for functions with very descriptive
            names, like </span><span>CmpCheckAndFixSecurityCellsRefcount</span><span>&nbsp;or
        </span><span>CmpPromoteSingleKeyFromParentKcbAndChildKeyNode</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">.<br></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c20 WuvYfpVbPj-c14"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"><a
                href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiSh03bRXYAIjyvdZP3lSfXk0rpn0MEMFsNzqr7Xjw-Vrt0LhsC7iXeEYkdxTAKH1koDNwIz0_VB55VrczcLHWd_TAlDG4K6dUFzHEx6kRmNTRdEyOCbRKFPZbFhlLRvwT9EWbt1drI_64OwPv3vmv5nNwYZLDRwihV4gEX_imvhdf05RkEjwtDMP0TmSQ/s527/image3.png"
                style="display: block; padding: 1em 0;text-align: center;"><img alt="Screenshot showing names and addresses" border="0" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiSh03bRXYAIjyvdZP3lSfXk0rpn0MEMFsNzqr7Xjw-Vrt0LhsC7iXeEYkdxTAKH1koDNwIz0_VB55VrczcLHWd_TAlDG4K6dUFzHEx6kRmNTRdEyOCbRKFPZbFhlLRvwT9EWbt1drI_64OwPv3vmv5nNwYZLDRwihV4gEX_imvhdf05RkEjwtDMP0TmSQ/s527/image3.png" style="max-height: 750px; max-width: 600px;" title="Screenshot showing names and addresses" /></a></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">The other type of information we can find in the
            kernel debug symbols are types: enums, structures and unions. However, there are two caveats. First, only
            some types are included in the PDBs, and it&#39;s not clear what criteria Microsoft uses to decide whether
            to publish them or not. My rough estimate is that ~50% of the registry types can be found there, mostly the
            fundamental ones. Secondly, even though the prototypes of some types are in the symbols, neither the
            function arguments nor local variables are annotated with their types, so it is still necessary to determine
            the corresponding types and manually annotate the variables for the decompiled output to make any sense.
            Nevertheless, having access to this information is still a huge help both in understanding code on a local
            level and also grasping the bigger picture.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">The structures that can be found in the public
            symbols are:</span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_uv05is8ytw5g-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>Hive descriptors
                (</span><span>HHIVE</span><span>, </span><span>CMHIVE</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">)
                and related structures</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>Hive bin and cell structures
                (</span><span>HBIN</span><span>, </span><span>CM_KEY_NODE</span><span>,
            </span><span>CM_KEY_VALUE</span><span>, </span><span>CM_KEY_SECURITY</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, ...)</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>Key object related structures
                (</span><span>CM_KEY_BODY</span><span>, </span><span>CM_KEY_CONTROL_BLOCK</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, ...)</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>Some transaction related structures
                (</span><span>CM_TRANS</span><span>, </span><span>CM_KCB_UOW</span><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, ...)</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>Some layered-key related structures
                (</span><span>CM_KCB_LAYER_INFO</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, ...)</span></li>
    </ul>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Meanwhile, the ones that are missing and need to
            be manually reconstructed are:</span></p>
    <ul style="padding: 0;" class="lst-kix_s50xc0ybvbc6-0 start">
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span>The parse context and path information
                structures (as used by </span><span>CmpParseKey</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">)</span>
        </li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Some
                transaction related structures (on-disk transaction log records, lightweight transaction object
                descriptors, ...)</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span
                class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Virtualization-related structures</span></li>
        <li style="margin-left: 46pt;" class="c13 c14 li-bullet-0"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Most
                layered-key related structures</span></li>
    </ul>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Most of the relevant type names start with
            &quot;CM&quot;, so it&#39;s easy to find them in the Local Types window in IDA:<br></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c14 WuvYfpVbPj-c20"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"><a
                href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiSYT2RhRyLkKdtFGJiJXHD52_fDQg4k0AnGtx-HjxYpkHMeuzFnxfObIKfckciDqx56QGbqFkW45lJvM4x2HAOUYwYW9Z-YY33xxBHXwEG1cRmnrg14w9dCNEr0c_6SI0lNMoih4nmIoz5QxfAYhYAkNl0CQYqROffqyAtclnsCRgUtj-Qg1TVAvD24zI/s729/image1.png"
                style="display: block; padding: 1em 0;text-align: center;"><img alt="Screenshot from IDA showing Local Types that start with CM" border="0" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEiSYT2RhRyLkKdtFGJiJXHD52_fDQg4k0AnGtx-HjxYpkHMeuzFnxfObIKfckciDqx56QGbqFkW45lJvM4x2HAOUYwYW9Z-YY33xxBHXwEG1cRmnrg14w9dCNEr0c_6SI0lNMoih4nmIoz5QxfAYhYAkNl0CQYqROffqyAtclnsCRgUtj-Qg1TVAvD24zI/s729/image1.png" style="max-height: 750px; max-width: 600px;" title="Screenshot from IDA showing Local Types that start with CM" /></a></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span><br></span><span>I would like to take this opportunity to thank Microsoft for making
            the symbols available for download, and encourage other vendors to do the same for their products.
            &#128578;</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <h3 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c18 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.neradbosl6or"><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c23 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Debug/Checked builds of Windows</span></h3>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Microsoft used to publish debug/checked builds of Windows (in addition to
            &quot;free&quot; builds) from Windows NT to early Windows 10. The </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/devtest/checked-and-free-build-differences&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260968815&amp;usg=AOvVaw0R8e3pRntzVwKO67ew2f2S">difference</a></span><span>&nbsp;between
            them was that the debug/checked builds had some compiler optimizations disabled, and they enabled extra
            debugging checks to identify internal system state inconsistencies as early as possible. The developers of
            kernel-mode drivers were encouraged to test them on debug/checked Windows builds before considering them as
            stable and shipping them to customers. Unfortunately, these special builds </span><span>have been
            discontinued</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;and don&#39;t exist anymore for the
            latest Windows 10 and 11.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>These old builds can be quite valuable in the context of reverse engineering, because
            the extra checks may reveal some invariants and assumptions that the code makes, but which are not obvious
            when looking at retail builds. What is more, the checks are often verbose and include calls to functions
            like </span><span>RtlAssert</span><span>, </span><span>DbgPrint</span><span>,
        </span><span>DbgPrintEx</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;etc., passing a textual
            representation of the failed assertion, the source code file name and/or the line number. These may disclose
            the names of variables, structure members, enums, constants and other types of information. Let&#39;s see
            some examples:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;\tImplausible size %lx\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v13);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;\tKey is bigger than containing cell.\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;invalid name starting with NULL on key %08lx\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">a3);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;invalid (ODD) name length on key %08lx\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">a3);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;\tNo key signature\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;\tData:%08lx - unallocated Data\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v20);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;Class:%08lx - Implausible size \n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v20);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;SecurityCell is HCELL_NIL for (%p,%08lx) !!!\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">a1,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v67);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;SecurityCell %08lx bad security for (%p,%08lx) !!!\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v86,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">a1,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v73);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;Root cell cannot be a symlink or predefined handle\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;invalid flags on root key %lx\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v31);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">24u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;\tWrong parent value.\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>The </span><span>CmpCheckKey</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;function is responsible for verifying the structural correctness
            of every key in a newly loaded hive, and for every problem it encounters, it prints a more or less verbose
            message. This can help us better understand what each of these checks is intended to accomplish.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;CmKCBToVirtualPath ==&gt; Could not get name even from parent KCB =
            %p!!!!\n&quot;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">a1);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>This message can be interpreted as some kind of a fallback mechanism failing when
            converting a registry path. It could indicate an interesting/brittle code construct, and indeed, the
            surrounding code did turn out to be affected by a 16-bit integer overflow and a resulting pool memory
            corruption (reported in Project Zero </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://bugs.chromium.org/p/project-zero/issues/detail?id%3D2341&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260973702&amp;usg=AOvVaw0SMrH7PXSKM-4BGHgURnhi">issue
                #2341</a></span><span>). In consequence, the entire block of code (including the vulnerability) was
            removed, as it was functionally redundant and didn&#39;t serve any practical purpose.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">RtlAssert(</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;(*VirtContext) &amp; CMP_VIRT_IDENTITY_RESTRICTED&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;minkernel\\ntos\\config\\cmveng.c&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">3554u</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">0i64</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>This single line of code in </span><span>CmpIsSystemEntity</span><span>&nbsp;reveals
            a few pieces of information: the name of the function argument (</span><span>VirtContext</span><span>), an
            internal name of a flag that is not documented in any other resources
            (</span><span>CMP_VIRT_IDENTITY_RESTRICTED</span><span>), and the source file name and line number of the
            expression (</span><span>minkernel\ntos\config\cmveng.c:3554</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">). Such information can be ported into our main disassembler database
            (such as an .idb) and later help us better understand other areas of code that use the same
            object/flags.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">DbgPrintEx(DPFLTR_CONFIG_ID,</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c11">22u</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">&quot;Error[1] %lx while processing CmLogRecActionDeleteKey\n&quot;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">,</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6 WuvYfpVbPj-c12">v12);</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>This and similar calls in </span><span>CmpDoReDoRecord</span><span>&nbsp;inform us of
            the internal names of the transaction record types (</span><span>CmLogRecActionCreateKey</span><span>,
        </span><span>CmLogRecActionDeleteKey</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;etc.), which again
            are not publicly mentioned anywhere else.</span></p>
    <h2 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c15 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.prue121m0dw1"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c1">Debugging and
            experimentation</span></h2>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Poking and prodding the registry of a running
            Windows system is the last way of learning about it that comes to my mind. In some sense it is a required
            step, because we can only get so far by reading static documentation and code. At some point, we will be
            forced to investigate the real memory mappings corresponding to the hives, explore the contents of in-memory
            registry objects, or verify that a specific function behaves the way we think it does. Thankfully, there are
            some tools that make it possible to peek into the internal registry state beyond what the standard utilities
            like Regedit allow. They are briefly described in the sections below.</span></p>
    <h3 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c14 WuvYfpVbPj-c18" id="h.pf8xpnqhvtr8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c23 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">Extended
            Regedit alternatives</span></h3>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>The built-in Regedit.exe utility offers quite basic functionality, and while it is
            adequate for most tinkering and system administration purposes, some third party developers have created
            custom registry editors with an extended set of options. </span><span>I haven&#39;t personally used them so
            I cannot attest to their quality, but they may offer some benefits to other
            researchers.</span><span>&nbsp;One example is </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://github.com/zodiacon/TotalRegistry&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260975682&amp;usg=AOvVaw0ndduj9AIaPe2f0oFloYdb">Total
                Registry</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">, whose main advantage is being able to
            browse the internal registry tree structure (rooted in \Registry) in addition to the standard high-level
            view with the five HKEY_* root keys.</span></p>
    <h3 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c18 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.czr9kptudml6"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c4 WuvYfpVbPj-c23">Process
            Monitor</span></h3>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/sysinternals/downloads/procmon&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260975937&amp;usg=AOvVaw0D49B5A87nI5PgYZ1zyFlE">Process
                Monitor</a></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;is a part of the Sysinternals suite of
            utilities, and is a widely known program for monitoring all file system, registry and process/thread
            activity in Windows in real time. Of course in this case, we are specifically interested in registry
            monitoring. For every operation taking place, we can see a corresponding line in the output window, which
            specifies the time, type of operation, originating process, registry key path, result of the operation and
            other details (all of this is highly configurable):</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c20 WuvYfpVbPj-c14"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"><a
                href="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgNPaOeCs3yHoc50mgrfml3iNk82jnY9Y2L4-3kzB7LkaGXQi0nCvH057-wjpSGkJfbsteRWLsBxsXxDjhwlif11c5wTLs3Nw2ZoM_hyphenhyphen21wZ1dUMwDS4jzqFgvIRt9N9y7gIx1lQpJNQ4FermJ2bQVI-QGSk8K1_9Kmtq3zM11rwGCVcHcJMnj2dq_HVqA/s770/image2.png"
                style="display: block; padding: 1em 0;text-align: center;"><img alt="Process monitor screenshot as described above" border="0" src="https://blogger.googleusercontent.com/img/b/R29vZ2xl/AVvXsEgNPaOeCs3yHoc50mgrfml3iNk82jnY9Y2L4-3kzB7LkaGXQi0nCvH057-wjpSGkJfbsteRWLsBxsXxDjhwlif11c5wTLs3Nw2ZoM_hyphenhyphen21wZ1dUMwDS4jzqFgvIRt9N9y7gIx1lQpJNQ4FermJ2bQVI-QGSk8K1_9Kmtq3zM11rwGCVcHcJMnj2dq_HVqA/s770/image2.png" style="max-height: 750px; max-width: 600px;" title="Process monitor screenshot as described above" /></a></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>ProcMon is a great tool for exploring what the registry is like as an interface, and
            how applications in the system use it. It is the most helpful when dealing with logical bugs, and attacking
            more privileged processes </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">through</span><span>&nbsp;the registry rather
            than attacking the registry implementation itself. For example, I used it to find a suitable exploitation
            primitive for Project Zero </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://bugs.chromium.org/p/project-zero/issues/detail?id%3D2492&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260976471&amp;usg=AOvVaw0Mcn_ZMdYfb6ANgKjm2UBy">issue
                #2492</a></span><span>, which allowed me to demonstrate that predefined keys were inherently insecure,
            leading to their deprecation. One of its advantages is that it works out-of-the-box without any special
            system configuration (other than the admin rights required to load a driver), and it&#39;s certainly a must
            have in a researcher&#39;s toolbox.</span></p>
    <h3 class="WuvYfpVbPj-c18 WuvYfpVbPj-c14" id="h.13ek42q2le6e"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c23 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&#128273;
            WinDbg and the !reg extension</span></h3>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>WinDbg attached as a kernel debugger to a test (virtual) machine is the ultimate tool
            to explore the inner workings of the Windows kernel. I have used it extensively at every step of my
            research, to analyze how the registry works, reproduce any bugs that I found, and develop reliable
            proof-of-concept exploits. While its standard debugger functionality is powerful enough for most tasks, it
            also comes with a dedicated </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7 WuvYfpVbPj-c26"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/debuggercmds/-reg&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260976834&amp;usg=AOvVaw1K4yYNzg1gLPti7AfjhOcn">!reg</a></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c7"><a
                href="https://www.google.com/url?q=https://learn.microsoft.com/en-us/windows-hardware/drivers/debuggercmds/-reg&amp;sa=D&amp;source=editors&amp;ust=1719419260976946&amp;usg=AOvVaw08nW9BbJ5E_x87T5eW8uAE">&nbsp;extension</a></span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;that automates the process of traversing registry-specific
            structures and presents them in an accessible way. The full list of its options is shown below:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">reg</span><span

    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>As we can see, the extension offers a wide selection of commands related to various
            components of the registry: hives, keys, values, security descriptors, transactions, notifications and so
            on. I have found many of them to be immensely useful, either on a regular basis (e.g. </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">querykey</span><span>, </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">kcb</span><span>,
        </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">hivelist</span><span>), or for more specialized tasks when experimenting with
            a particular feature (e.g. </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">translist</span><span>, </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">uowlist</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;for
            transactions).</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>The best way to discover its potential is to see it in action on a specific example.
            I used a Windows 11 guest system for this purpose. Let&#39;s query an existing
        </span><span>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\Software\DefaultUserEnvironment</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;key to find out more about it:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span></span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">kd&gt;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">!reg</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">querykey</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">\Registry\Machine\Software\DefaultUserEnvironment</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c3"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c3"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">Found</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">KCB</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">=</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">ffff888788731ad0</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">::</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">\REGISTRY\MACHINE\SOFTWARE\DEFAULTUSERENVIRONMENT</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c3"></span></p>

        </span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">000001e6ed0334b4</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c3"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">[ValueType]</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">[ValueName]</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">[ValueData]</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">REG_EXPAND_SZ</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">Path</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">%USERPROFILE%\AppData\Local\Microsoft\WindowsApps;</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">REG_EXPAND_SZ</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">TEMP</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; </span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">%USERPROFILE%\AppData\Local\Temp</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">REG_EXPAND_SZ</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">TMP</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c6">&nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;
            &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp; &nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c0">%USERPROFILE%\AppData\Local\Temp</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p><br>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Here, we have referenced the key by its internal NT object manager registry path
            starting with </span><span>\Registry</span><span>. The relation between the high-level paths known from
            Regedit / the Registry API and the internal paths used by the kernel will be detailed in a future post
            &ndash; for now, we just need to know that these paths are equivalent. We can learn a few things from the
            command output: the key is cached in memory and the KCB (</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">Key Control
            Block</span><span>, represented by the </span><span>CM_KEY_CONTROL_BLOCK</span><span>&nbsp;structure) is
            located at address </span><span>0xffff888788731ad0</span><span>. The address of the SOFTWARE hive descriptor
            is </span><span>0xffff88877af5c000</span><span>, and that&#39;s where the
        </span><span>HHIVE</span><span>&nbsp;</span><span>/ </span><span>CMHIVE</span><span>&nbsp;structures are stored.
        </span><span>HHIVE</span><span>&nbsp;is the first member of </span><span>CMHIVE</span><span>&nbsp;at offset 0,
            hence why their addresses line up, similar to how the </span><span>KPROCESS</span><span>&nbsp;/
        </span><span>EPROCESS</span><span>&nbsp;structures work. Furthermore, the key node
            (</span><span>CM_KEY_NODE</span><span>), the definitive representation of a key within the hive file, is
            mapped at address </span><span>0x1e6ed0334b4</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">. You may
            notice that this is a user-mode address, and that&#39;s because in modern versions of Windows, hive files
            are generally operated on via section-based mappings within the user address space of a thin
            &quot;Registry&quot; process (you can find it in Task Manager). Lastly, we can see that the key has three
            values and we are provided with their types, names and data.</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c8"><span>Next, we can </span><span>use</span><span>&nbsp;</span><span
            class="WuvYfpVbPj-c2">!reg kcb</span><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4">&nbsp;to learn more about the
            key based on its cached KCB data:</span></p>
    <p class="WuvYfpVbPj-c9 WuvYfpVbPj-c10"><span class="WuvYfpVbPj-c5 WuvYfpVbPj-c4"></span></p>
